        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=classic-of-the-month">classic-of-the-month</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=out-of-europe">out-of-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3273251790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1555342851737197415"><b>"In the old man’s time the miner lived in the mine like a brute, like a machine for extracting coal, always under the earth, with ears and eyes stopped to outward events. So the rich, who governed, found it easy to sell him and buy him, and to devour his flesh; he did not even know what was going on."</b><br/><br/><i>Germinal</i> is a book I've been intending to read for a long time. I'm not sure now was the best time because it's rather depressing, but it is also a good book and well worth reading.<br/><br/>Emile Zola wrote </span>
  <span id="freeText1555342851737197415" style="display:none"><b>"In the old man’s time the miner lived in the mine like a brute, like a machine for extracting coal, always under the earth, with ears and eyes stopped to outward events. So the rich, who governed, found it easy to sell him and buy him, and to devour his flesh; he did not even know what was going on."</b><br /><br /><i>Germinal</i> is a book I've been intending to read for a long time. I'm not sure now was the best time because it's rather depressing, but it is also a good book and well worth reading.<br /><br />Emile Zola wrote for the unheard masses, those suffering and powerless people whom the rich exploited. To those without voices he gave a voice. <br /><br />In <i>Germinal</i>, Zola tells the story of workers at coal mines in France. The suffering he describes as they spend their lives toiling away deep in the bowels of the earth is harrowing. The rich owners pay them barely enough to survive on and so they are always hungry. Children too are sent to work in the mines in order to supplement their family's meager income. The mines are dangerous places and many are injured, leaving the family to support a person who is unable to contribute. Of course, this was not just the case in France, but in many other countries as well. <br /><br />Because these poor people have little to no access to education, and because they have little time or energy to think, they have scant hope of changing their situation.  Etienne Lantier was a man who lost his job on the railway and because he was somewhat educated, he saw what the miners could not and gave them hope that together they had power. He organized a strike that had devastating consequences but which also gave hope to the hopeless. <br /><br />Zola paints a vivid portrait of this time and place and these miners and I was immersed in their lives, at times hoping with them, at times feeling their despair. I couldn't help comparing the rich owners to the corporations of today that are always striving to chip away at workers' rights. If allowed, most would have unsafe working conditions for their employees, work them longer and longer hours with less pay, and have little to no recourse for any suffering or injury they might sustain. Unfortunately, workers rights are being eroded, at least here in the US, and people have little or no time to think, and no hope that they have a voice or power to change things for the better. And then there is the developing world where rich corporations exploit the poor, basically using them as slave labor and granting them as few rights as workers had in Zola's time. Would Zola be shocked if he was alive today? Perhaps, perhaps not, but he no doubt would have striven to give the people a voice. <br /><br />My one complaint about this book is its sexism. The most glaring example is how when 15 year old Catherine is raped, Zola explains that her no didn't mean no and girls only said no when they really meant yes. Right after she is assaulted and her virginity stolen from her, we are told Catherine and her rapist get up and go about their way, "they stopped twice beside the canal, three times along the pit-bank, very cheerful now and occupied with little tender games."  Yes, you read that right! Catherine is brutally raped but, because she really did "want it" in spite of pleading with Chaval to stop, she jumps right up and goes skipping down the lane with her rapist, cuddling and happily playing little games. What the hell, Zola??? I was infuriated with that. <br /><br />Poor young Catherine then pairs up with Chaval, thinking she deserves no better than a man who raped her (oh wait, it wasn't <i>really</i> rape!) and who beats her on a regular basis. In some ways, her situation echoes that of all the miners and so perhaps Zola was using this symbolically. I get that, but not how Zola used the disgusting stereotype that a woman never really means no. A woman's "yes" means "yes" but her "no" also means "yes". Zola spoke for the worker's rights but not for women's rights. Yes, he was a product of his time, but it was still infuriating.<br /><br />That bitch aside, and it's a big bitch, I still am glad to have read this book. There are some tedious parts as there often are in classics. However, it is brilliantly written and though at times depressing, Zola reminds us there is always hope. Once the seed of thought has been planted and begins to germinate, people can find their own voices and power and, by working together, change things for the better. <br /><br /><i>April 2020 classic-of-the-month</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="1555342851737197415" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_791260262" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/791260262" />
    <a title="Greg Brozeit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26552534-greg-brozeit"><img alt="Greg Brozeit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544324486p2/26552534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/791260262?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg Brozeit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg Brozeit" href="/user/show/26552534-greg-brozeit">Greg Brozeit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26552534-greg-brozeit?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26552534-greg-brozeit?shelf=zola">zola</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer791260262" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9316592771665944892">Rereading <i>Germinal</i> during the self-isolation/lockdown of the COVID-19 pandemic, after more than 37 years when I first read it in college—the best book I read as a student—is one of those things that makes me wonder if there is something mystical in the world after all. I’ve been struggling with writing a review. I had quotes and paragraphs lined up to put together into an eloquent argument for why this may be the most relevant novel to explain any place and any time in history even though it is </span>
  <span id="freeText9316592771665944892" style="display:none">Rereading <i>Germinal</i> during the self-isolation/lockdown of the COVID-19 pandemic, after more than 37 years when I first read it in college—the best book I read as a student—is one of those things that makes me wonder if there is something mystical in the world after all. I’ve been struggling with writing a review. I had quotes and paragraphs lined up to put together into an eloquent argument for why this may be the most relevant novel to explain any place and any time in history even though it is set in a northern French mine in the late 1860s. I was going to write about how the characters, including the mine itself, La Voreux, with its glowing entrance looking like the eyes of an emerging demon from hell, and the work horses in the mine, Battle and Trumpet, who, like every character in the novel were so timelessly archetypical.<br /><br />I was going to write: Human rights are only rights if people can exercise them freely, without fear of retribution from reactionary forces. What, for example, does the right to privacy mean if one never has the opportunity to be private? What does the right of life mean if one can’t afford to eat or have shelter? What does the right of assembly mean if it risks one’s livelihood and freedom? What does anything mean if one can’t live in safety and dignity? What do any rights mean if people in power and civil authority exploit powerless, easily manipulated people whether because of economics, race, ethnicity, or historical grievances? When does the point come when people who have been oppressed are triggered to have their collective grievances explode? The answers to those questions are basically the story of <i>Germinal</i>, Zola’s masterpiece.<br /><br />But then I got stuck, my review was becoming a recitation of the book itself. Then the murder of George Floyd at the knee of a policeman occurred in Minneapolis. The hidden, festering scars of America’s original sin are bursting. And all I could think about was, “How can I relate this to our times?” Then this morning, I made a comment on the blog of education scholar Diane Ravitch in response to a powerful essay she wrote about the violence that is taking place in this nation as I write this. (Every American should read her books <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1864227374" rel="nofollow noopener">The Death and Life of the American School System</a>
</i> and <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/788500601" rel="nofollow noopener">Reign of Error</a>
</i>.) And I realized after writing it that I wrote my review of <i>Germinal</i>:<br /><br />The first three words of our national governing document are “We the People…” It does not start with “In the beginning God created…” That’s a distinction too many Americans do not understand. “We the people…” takes many forms, sometimes in the ugly actions of mobs, something the framers feared most. But sometimes mobs define the path forward. This was something the framers chose to ignore. Could it not be argued, for example, that the Boston Tea Party was a mob? Was it not the spark that conceived this nation? Can it not be so now?<br /><br />A classic of French literature, Emile Zola’s <i>Germinal</i> is a perfect metaphor for our times. When coal miners who have been abused for generations are sparked to act by an incident that is frightening analogous to our times, “It was as though an ulcer of resentment had been growing within them, a poisonous abscess, which had finally burst. Year after year of hunger had made them ravenous for a fest of massacre and destruction.” Yet when all was said and done, “they…lapsed back into their habitual state of inbred acquiescence, fearful of the morrow and still preferring to toe the line.” Is that going to be our fate as well? Will a leaderless regime with a sinister agenda condemn us to a similar outcome?<br /><br />This is a time for “We the People…” to take the lead, learn the lessons this episode in history is trying to teach us, internalize them, and act in a constructive, collective way of moving forward. There will be no God to create a rational order. No sentient person would think this. The unprecedented convergence of the stark exposition of racism combined with the worldwide crisis of the pandemic calls on grassroots engagement to find a path forward. Forces that want to exterminate “We the People…” including Putinistas and neo-Nazis in the U.S. are gleeful; this is everything for which they hoped. Are “We the People…” up to the challenge? Twenty years from now, one of three scenarios will exist. Will we have addressed some of the injustice that has been historically ingrained in our nation? Will we retreat to the comfort of inbred acquiescence? Or will the nation be driven to become a repeat of the Second Empire of France, with an unelected dictatorship borne of dominance of the financial and political elite to create even more inequalities and a permanent underclass to serve them?<br /><br />The answer is not in the stars. For better or worse, it is in “We the People…”<br /><br />Read this book. And if you've read it before, reread it again, especially now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9316592771665944892" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_63889420" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63889420" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2447803-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246308632p2/2447803.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63889420?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/2447803-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63889420" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7154899392838599616">Felt like reading a Naturlist, and I remembered Zola. Germinal was the only Zola novel on the library shelf, and I chose it merely in deferrence to the author. Little did I know that many critics believe Germinal is one of the 10 best French novels ever written. <br/><br/>I like stories where people are ground down by nature--poverty, weather, work conditions, hunger--and the lower economic demographic is forced to suffer and survive. The Industrial Revolution offered so many ways to catalogue the sufferi</span>
  <span id="freeText7154899392838599616" style="display:none">Felt like reading a Naturlist, and I remembered Zola. Germinal was the only Zola novel on the library shelf, and I chose it merely in deferrence to the author. Little did I know that many critics believe Germinal is one of the 10 best French novels ever written. <br /><br />I like stories where people are ground down by nature--poverty, weather, work conditions, hunger--and the lower economic demographic is forced to suffer and survive. The Industrial Revolution offered so many ways to catalogue the suffering of humanity at the hands of the machine, in this case the coal industry in northern France, 1866.<br /><br />The writing is wonderful, and you can tell Zola was on a mission to pit capital versus labor. The language he uses to describe the coal shaft is graphic, visceral, and unrelenting. The labor, the people of Montsou, live in startling poverty and are subjected to health and safety dangers, rendering a man in his 50's an ancient relic. I can't imagine poverty so overwhelming that you sell articles of clothing for bread, nor a market willing to take those rags and be able to turn a profit. Capital, the mine shareholders and finaciers, are represented by mine management, and Zola uses allusions to the owners of the mine as some far-off, untouchable class of humans that feed off labor's blood, sweat and tears.<br /><br />Some of the more interesting characters are drawn a bit thin, but the main players are well developed. For me, Zola's best writing occurs during intense crisis. It flows so naturally and powerfully, that my reading speeds up, yet my comprehension is increased. I'm now increasingly interested in Zola's 20-part series documenting the hale and harrows of one French family.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7154899392838599616" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_42067638" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42067638" />
    <a title="Leftbanker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/352711-leftbanker"><img alt="Leftbanker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530584670p2/352711.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42067638?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leftbanker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leftbanker" href="/user/show/352711-leftbanker">Leftbanker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/352711-leftbanker?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/352711-leftbanker?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42067638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14952604611395987132"><i>Moi, je vois autrement. Je n’ai guère de souci et de beauté et de perfection. Je me moque des grands siècles. Je n’ai souci que de vie, de lutte, de fièvre.</i> -Émile Zola<br/><br/>Zola is the supreme novelist, at least how I interpret that vocation. Like Dickens, Zola went out and studied France and her people for inspiration while Proust sat in a cork-lined room and dreamed up all of his stories in his head. I'll take journalism over the human imagination any day. Germinal is the essence of this style of w</span>
  <span id="freeText14952604611395987132" style="display:none"><i>Moi, je vois autrement. Je n’ai guère de souci et de beauté et de perfection. Je me moque des grands siècles. Je n’ai souci que de vie, de lutte, de fièvre.</i> -Émile Zola<br /><br />Zola is the supreme novelist, at least how I interpret that vocation. Like Dickens, Zola went out and studied France and her people for inspiration while Proust sat in a cork-lined room and dreamed up all of his stories in his head. I'll take journalism over the human imagination any day. Germinal is the essence of this style of writing. I like how Zola doesn't romanticize the life of the miners as he shows the terrible conditions of their work. I love how Zola trained his eye on an aspect of his society that desperately needed change and tried to effect that change. One of the culminations of this new power of the pen was his activism in the Dreyfuss Affair.<br /><br />I don't have much use for the other school of novelist represented by Proust. This seems to be the one followed by so much of the current literary output in America. Whenever I read a synopsis of a novel by one of the most favored American writers and the story deals with either a writer or a university professor, my eyes roll back in my head so hard I almost fall over backwards. I'm talking about Bellow, Irving, Updike, Cheever, Roth, Carol Oates, to name a few of these types who can't seem to be bothered to leave their university jobs and cocktail parties to find something worth writing about. Instead, they obsess over the little lives of intellectuals—sort of like Woody Allen and his childish fixation with relationships. Go out and learn something new. <br /><br />They always say that you should write about what you know. The problem is most of these writers don't know much. Their knowledge of politics and economics is embarrassing. Do some homework before writing and stop relying on your tired imagination. When I read something I can almost always tell if the author is a university professor. They almost all have the same lifeless style. They only write for other academics.<br /><br />I am working hard this year to improve my French so that I can read this classic in the original.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14952604611395987132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_24443793" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<div id="review_526052955" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/526052955" />
    <a title="Mikey B." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1484029-mikey-b"><img alt="Mikey B." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578234137p2/1484029.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/526052955?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mikey B." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mikey B." href="/user/show/1484029-mikey-b">Mikey B.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1484029-mikey-b?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1484029-mikey-b?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer526052955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8394703992982014799">** Some spoilers within **<br/><br/>An extremely intriguing story that moves along with several sub-plots – all of them well interconnected. The style is very social oriented – in this case exploring the lives of coal miners in the north of France. It resembles Dickens who was concerned with both social issues and class issues; but unlike Dickens, who was very puritanical; the sexual passages in “Germinal” are really quite forthright – for instance women have menstrual cycles.<br/><br/>Zola, I would say, is somewhe</span>
  <span id="freeText8394703992982014799" style="display:none">** Some spoilers within **<br /><br />An extremely intriguing story that moves along with several sub-plots – all of them well interconnected. The style is very social oriented – in this case exploring the lives of coal miners in the north of France. It resembles Dickens who was concerned with both social issues and class issues; but unlike Dickens, who was very puritanical; the sexual passages in “Germinal” are really quite forthright – for instance women have menstrual cycles.<br /><br />Zola, I would say, is somewhere between Dickens and Orwell. There is a proletariat here – and it is not an idolized proletariat. They engage in sexual relations upon reaching puberty, and after that they beat their spouses. The depictions of starvation, of the work in coal mines, of the rallies of the workers – are all extremely poignant and stunning. This book is a page turner.<br /><br />“Germinal” is more event driven than character driven. Sometimes the characters seem more like “types” – representing a socialist, an anarchist, a working class person... Zola makes the events derive a life of their own – like the strikes – which propels people in unexpected ways. This is very much true to life, where once a course is decided upon, predictions and theories go out the window.<br /><br />In some ways women are cast as down-trodden and suitable only for sexual gratification and motherhood; but perhaps this is a reflection of the era. The lives of the women in this story are sordid – they have no hope of escaping the enslavement of raising several children on subsistence wages. The upper class women seem intent on acquiring power through the use of sex. Catherine, however, is probably the most complex character in the story – rivaling Etienne. At times Catherine has an aura of saintliness about her – we want her to break out of her cage and we sense her desire to do so. But in the end, she too becomes trapped. <br /><br />This was the first time I have read a book of Zola and it was a very positive experience.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8394703992982014799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17377234117405331496" style="display:none">I wonder, just how much suffering can the laboring masses endure? <div id="review_197291040" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/197291040" />
    <a title="Moon Rose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5189565-moon-rose"><img alt="Moon Rose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597367219p2/5189565.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/197291040?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Moon Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Moon Rose" href="/user/show/5189565-moon-rose">Moon Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/12023824-germinal">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=2011-shelf">2011-shelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer197291040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4985845388433153761">δ∝•☜<u>
  <b>THE CLAMOR FOR SOCIAL EQUITY</b>
</u>☞•∝δ<br/><br/><i>Germinal</i> refers to the season of spring, the time of renewal when the seed of life starts to sprout again from the ground, germinating hope after the long dormancy of winter.<br/><br/>Émile Zola symbolically refers to this spring of hope as the wretched lives of the coal miners, amidst the sour inflictions of deprivation, leading to their depraved lives, slowly awaken from their long years of passive obedience, allowing them to see a picture of a better life as it h</span>
  <span id="freeText4985845388433153761" style="display:none">δ∝•☜<u>
  <b>THE CLAMOR FOR SOCIAL EQUITY</b>
</u>☞•∝δ<br /><br /><i>Germinal</i> refers to the season of spring, the time of renewal when the seed of life starts to sprout again from the ground, germinating hope after the long dormancy of winter.<br /><br />Émile Zola symbolically refers to this spring of hope as the wretched lives of the coal miners, amidst the sour inflictions of deprivation, leading to their depraved lives, slowly awaken from their long years of passive obedience, allowing them to see a picture of a better life as it hustles the wind of revolt to penetrate their already blacken skins, giving them courage to rise against their masters, the sated gods who greedily gnaw their flesh to the bone, robbing them blindly of their rightful share.<br /><br />This sudden awakening of the coal miners from their deep dormant slumber serves as a foreboding intensifier to the narrative as it rolls with a sonorous rumble of a dreadful drum. It moves with deepening urgency, filling the pages with suspense and anticipation as it pulsates with deafening vibrancy and ferocity, shaking the primordial placidness in the hearts of the coal miners like a catastrophic earthquake. Its violent trembles are captured in verbatim by Zola's meticulous descriptive style as the cracks that open the earth with large fissures are deeply felt, visibly letting out a hot boiling steam of wrath that rages to destroy everything in its midst. <br /><br />This fiery elemental force that foretells the destructive nature of a revolution is what Zola vividly recreates in <i>Germinal</i>, capturing humanity's fiercest cry for justice. As the clamor to attain social equity in the midst of a tumultuous anger, giddily allow them to succumb to their instinctive desire of retribution in the name of universal happiness, encumbering their divine pursuits of universal peace, as it derails them towards the true meaning of justice. 佛月球 Будда Луны</span>
  <a data-text-id="4985845388433153761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_577131465" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/577131465" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18948588-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569598891p2/18948588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/577131465?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/18948588-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=read-again">read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=five-star-novels">five-star-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=r-r">r-r</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=zola">zola</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer577131465" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15599115669377576611"><i> Germinal</i> is the thirteenth novel in Émile Zola's twenty-volume series <i>Les Rougon-Macquart.</i> Just typing that made me want to go dig it out and re-read it. I loved this book. It was so depressing and horrible and everybody was so miserable I don't know how you couldn't like this book. :-) It was published in 1885 and has been called Zola's masterpiece. I have no idea if it is his masterpiece because I've read lots of other Zola novels that I like just as much. But it was an awesome book, if you l</span>
  <span id="freeText15599115669377576611" style="display:none"><i> Germinal</i> is the thirteenth novel in Émile Zola's twenty-volume series <i>Les Rougon-Macquart.</i> Just typing that made me want to go dig it out and re-read it. I loved this book. It was so depressing and horrible and everybody was so miserable I don't know how you couldn't like this book. :-) It was published in 1885 and has been called Zola's masterpiece. I have no idea if it is his masterpiece because I've read lots of other Zola novels that I like just as much. But it was an awesome book, if you like being depressed that is. It has been filmed five times, and has had two television productions, which I guess would also count as filming. I've seen nothing of any of this whether you call it film or not.<br /><br />The novel is set in the 1860s in a mining community in northern France and I would say it is a cry of protest against the oppression and misery of the poor miners and their families, it should be if it isn't. This novel will follow Etienne Lantier, our "hero", on a journey through the working mining community that brings him face to face with violence and despair and also total misery. Etienne is the son of Gervaise Macquart, the alcoholic at the center of L'Assommoir, an earlier novel in the Rougon-Macquart series. (that was also a really cool book.) Etienne arrives in town looking for work as an mechanic, he has been sacked from his previous job on the railways for assaulting a superior, a dumb thing to do, he now befriends a veteran miner who finds him somewhere to stay and gets him a job pushing the carts down the pit. It isn't as nice of him as it sounds. <br /><br />While this is going on, Étienne also falls for Catherine, also employed pushing carts in the mines. At first Etienne mistakes 15-year-old Catherine, for a boy, because she is so small and her puberty has been delayed by the physical strain of her job pushing a cart at the bottom of the pit. When he realizes she is both an attractive girl and his friend, helping him learn the job, sharing her food with him, he falls for her. <br /><br />The lives of the miners and their families is horrible. It is one of severe poverty and oppression, as their working and living conditions continue to worsen throughout the novel; there are horrible conditions in the mines; falling rocks, bad air, explosive gases. The miners finally decide to go on strike and violence erupts. There is nothing good about these people's lives. This book is filled with lots of people. There are lots of mining people, of course, there are mining men and mining women and mining children. There are the owners and the managers and the people who have been made invalids by working in those mines. There are bar owners, who seem to end up with most of the miners money. The men beat their wives, the wives are miserable, so are the children. There is some hope in the book, but you have to search for it.<br /><br />Just writing this I'm sitting here thinking "who will want to read it after this?". But it was amazing. The story just seemed so real. I'll never be done reading this book. Here are two of my favorite quotes just to prove there is some humor in there:<br /><br /> <i> “You’re better off on your own, there’s nobody to disagree with.”</i><br /><br /> <i>“When the men and the girl came back from the pit, they’d have to eat again; for nobody had yet discovered how to live without eating, unfortunately."</i><br /><br />That's all I have to say about this sad, depressing book, it's time to move on. Happy Reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15599115669377576611" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10542742211598588387" style="display:none">Rating this book is a difficult thing.<br><br>It took me a while to read it, I knew that before starting it, Zola is a big deal. Long descriptions, unnecessary parts, complicated way to say what he wants. But in the end, the meaning and the message is always deep and strong. <br><br><b>Rating : 3,5</b><br><br>As I said I was about to give up on this book every two seconds, I was like "hold on, it is not that bad"<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1429271316ra/14562549.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Let's be serious for a minute. <br>I did liked this book. I already studied this part of history in school, but Zola bring us to another level. I always admired the amount of work he puts on his books, all the researches he does. I knew that miners had a poor and tough life, but it is one thing to know, it is another thing to SEE this misery. Misery is even a to soft word. Their conditions are just AWFUL.<br>Indeed Zola's precision make us apprehend this part of history more clearly.<br><br>I loved Maheu, I think this was the most wise and complex character (even though I disliked his wife). <br>And I pitied his daughter Catherine so much, I think this is the worst, seeing how young she is, and how nice she tries to be. And that often goes bad for her because of that.<br>I think that the main character is supposed to be Etienne but he is so secondary to me, he is kind of a witness, newly arrived to that life where some lived for a while. <br><br>Before rating and reviewing this book, I decided to watch the movie (the 1993 version with Gerard Depardieu) and it was good, very good even. Close to the book and I recommend it to you guys, even though I don't like Gerard Depardieu, it was even more interesting to actually see that misery. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1122741133921282395">The wobbly cages descending into the pit, miners half-naked toiling in the scorching darkness of the mine’s galleries, the veins bursting and flooding the passages, the meager wages the miners receive at the end of the day, the wives desperately scouring for gruel each meal, the parents giving their daughters to the grocer to get flour and sugar; all recounted in a calmly detached voice. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1409441282i/10979537._SY540_.jpg' width='170' height='200' alt='Courrières Mine Disaster' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Courrières Mine Disaster<br/><br/>Etienne, a vagrant worker, joined the fraternity and dissatisfied with the inhuman da</span>
  <span id="freeText1122741133921282395" style="display:none">The wobbly cages descending into the pit, miners half-naked toiling in the scorching darkness of the mine’s galleries, the veins bursting and flooding the passages, the meager wages the miners receive at the end of the day, the wives desperately scouring for gruel each meal, the parents giving their daughters to the grocer to get flour and sugar; all recounted in a calmly detached voice. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1409441282i/10979537._SY540_.jpg" width="170" height="200" alt="Courrières Mine Disaster" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Courrières Mine Disaster<br><br>Etienne, a vagrant worker, joined the fraternity and dissatisfied with the inhuman daily drudges and ambitious to rise above these defeated and resigned miners organized them into a union and led the strike. But the strike revealed as much the indifference of the owners and managers as the ignorance and violence of the miners. After many lives perished, many families shattered, many mines destroyed, the strike failed, the miners returned to work and Etienne left. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1409440697i/10979482._SY540_.jpg" width="170" height="200" alt="Emile Zola" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Emile Zola<br><br>In Germinal, Zola harmonized the detached narrative voice with the miner’s sub-human existence and their potential for gratuitous evil to evoke a chilling sonata that would haunt the reader long after the novel’s conclusion. </span>

<div id="review_2108868187" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2108868187" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70459127-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592666421p2/70459127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2108868187?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/70459127-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70459127-tara?shelf=1001-list">1001-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2108868187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3641657344001141385"><i>Germinal</i> thoroughly described the social and political climate of a coal miners’ uprising in northern France in the 1860s. The book was admittedly slow to start, but once it finally got going, it was truly engrossing…up until the last 80 pages or so, when it anticlimactically trickled off to a fairly lackluster ending. Also, I could have done without the “dramatic” love triangle, which didn’t really add much to the book in my opinion.<br/><br/>At its best, however, the book was damned exciting. The unbear</span>
  <span id="freeText3641657344001141385" style="display:none"><i>Germinal</i> thoroughly described the social and political climate of a coal miners’ uprising in northern France in the 1860s. The book was admittedly slow to start, but once it finally got going, it was truly engrossing…up until the last 80 pages or so, when it anticlimactically trickled off to a fairly lackluster ending. Also, I could have done without the “dramatic” love triangle, which didn’t really add much to the book in my opinion.<br /><br />At its best, however, the book was damned exciting. The unbearably tense atmosphere of repressed rage Zola cultivated, and the explosive way in which he unleashed it, was nothing short of masterful. He had me so worked up at one point that I was eager to hurl bricks too, and my heart shouted and jeered in sympathetic ferocity along with my fellow strikers. That said, I must admit that I was listening to Arch Enemy at the time, and their music tends to make me angry and throw-y anyway. After all, death metal isn’t really noted for its <i>calming</i> effects.<br /><br />One thing’s for certain: after reading this book, I know I’ll never look at a Hot Dog on a Stick eatery in quite the same way. I won’t be able to read the phrase “stick it to the man” in as innocent a fashion as before either :O</span>
  <a data-text-id="3641657344001141385" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_278880357" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/278880357" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/278880357?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/437141.Germinal">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer278880357" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15379997896919028779"><b>4 and 1/2 stars</b><br/><br/>I'm presently in an online group discussing this book, which is probably the reason I don't feel like writing a proper review. <br/><br/>This is my second Zola and I admired it as much as I did my first, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/92967.L_Assommoir__The_Dram_Shop___Les_Rougon_Macquart___7_' title='L'Assommoir (The Dram Shop) (Les Rougon-Macquart, #7) by Émile Zola' rel='nofollow noopener'>L'assommoir</a>, even if I subjectively liked the latter a bit more. The structure of the novel and the way Zola handed the complexity of the issues through the eyes of his main character, Etienne (son of the main character in "L'assommoir"), is impressive.<br/><br/>The group scenes are tense, thrilling,</span>
  <span id="freeText15379997896919028779" style="display:none"><b>4 and 1/2 stars</b><br /><br />I'm presently in an online group discussing this book, which is probably the reason I don't feel like writing a proper review. <br /><br />This is my second Zola and I admired it as much as I did my first, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/92967.L_Assommoir__The_Dram_Shop___Les_Rougon_Macquart___7_" title="L'Assommoir (The Dram Shop) (Les Rougon-Macquart, #7) by Émile Zola" rel="nofollow noopener">L'assommoir</a>, even if I subjectively liked the latter a bit more. The structure of the novel and the way Zola handed the complexity of the issues through the eyes of his main character, Etienne (son of the main character in "L'assommoir"), is impressive.<br /><br />The group scenes are tense, thrilling, feverish and psychologically astute, as is the portrayal of hardships certain individuals endure, though the handling of one incident I felt was perhaps gratuitous and then treated as a throwaway. Though the book was written in the late-19th century, its language and issues are as gritty and contemporary as they would be in a book of today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15379997896919028779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SfPiD9XmovB41jumG91ajy+ryynpbXBMZkrQ4ZpqmChz1YvdbrhrmN/9j8fsKFBQ+fQs4Sfg/is9NJZQVylqsg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BXNwmRSI49SjoyuSb0Ri6Pw6kSJz553s8IYTUNflqqU/VRlLr9YqvASIn/OYsWg3KmV26r1qE4ur+FXhGqZYPw==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_780581'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/780581.Bel_Ami?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Bel-Ami<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/18791.Guy_de_Maupassant?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Guy de Maupassant<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.85 avg rating &mdash; 29,545 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1885\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer7606418881191318461\">Guy de Maupassant\'s scandalous tale of an opportunistic young man corrupted by the allure of power, \"Bel-Ami\" is translated with an introduction by Douglas Parmee in \"Penguin Classics\". Young, attractive and very ambitious, George Duroy, known to his<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7606418881191318461\" style=\"display:none\">Guy de Maupassant\'s scandalous tale of an opportunistic young man corrupted by the allure of power, &quot;Bel-Ami&quot; is translated with an introduction by Douglas Parmee in &quot;Penguin Classics&quot;. Young, attractive and very ambitious, George Duroy, known to his admirers as Bel-Ami, is offered a job as a journalist on La Vie francaise and soon makes a great success of his new career. But he also comes face to face with the realities of the corrupt society in which he lives - the sleazy colleagues, the manipulative mistresses, and wily financiers - and swiftly learns to become an arch-seducer, blackmailer and social climber in a world where love is only a means to an end. Written when Maupassant was at the height of his powers, &quot;Bel-Ami&quot; is a novel of great frankness and cynicism, but it is also infused with the sheer joy of life - depicting the scenes and characters of Paris in the belle epoque with wit, sensitivity, and humanity. Douglas Parmee\'s translation captures all the vigour and vitality of Maupassant\'s novel. His introduction explores the similarities between Bel-Ami and Maupassant himself and demonstrates the skill with which the author depicts his large cast of characters and the French society of the An ardent devourer of sentimental novels, she longs for passion and seeks escape in fantasies of high romance, in voracious spending and, eventually, in adultery. But even her affairs bring her disappointment, and when real life continues to fail to live up to her romantic expectations, the consequences are devastating. Flaubert\'s erotically charged and psychologically acute portrayal of Emma Bovary caused a moral outcry on its publication in 1857. It was deemed so lifelike that many women claimed they were the model for his heroine; but Flaubert insisted: \'Madame Bovary, c\'est moi.\'\n\nThis modern translation by Flaubert\'s biographer, Geoffrey Wall, retains all the delicacy and precision of the French original. In the service of Mlle de Varandeuil, Germinie <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9396231520435116197\" style=\"display:none\">Orphaned at the age of four, Germinie Lacerteux is forced to enter service in Paris in order to pay her way, in a story that paints a vision of 19th-century Paris haunted by poverty, death, and despair. In the service of Mlle de Varandeuil, Germinie realizes that if she wishes to remain employed, she faces an unmarried life. Such a frustration of her maternal instincts engenders a deep devotion to her niece; Germinie is crushed when the girl is removed by her parents. Many relationships of the same mold ensue, the heroine each time forging loving bonds that are broken. In the face of such drudgery, the Goncourt brothers reveal the value of such simple pleasures as a walk in the countryside; and through the story of Germinie Lacerteux’s life, they examine questions of the individual’s power—or powerlessness—to control his or her own fate.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"9396231520435116197\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4750._mile_Zola">About Émile Zola</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4750._mile_Zola"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1544927603p3/4750.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4750._mile_Zola">Émile Zola
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,110
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16397027481251126548">Émile François Zola was an influential French novelist, the most important example of the literary school of naturalism, and a major figure in the political liberalization of France.<br/><br/>More than half of Zola's novels were part of a set of 20 books collectively known as Les Rougon-Macquart. Unlike Balzac who in the midst of his literary career resynthesized his work into La Comédie Humaine, Zola from </span>
  <span id="freeText16397027481251126548" style="display:none">Émile François Zola was an influential French novelist, the most important example of the literary school of naturalism, and a major figure in the political liberalization of France.<br /><br />More than half of Zola's novels were part of a set of 20 books collectively known as Les Rougon-Macquart. Unlike Balzac who in the midst of his literary career resynthesized his work into La Comédie Humaine, Zola from the start at the age of 28 had thought of the complete layout of the series. Set in France's Second Empire, the series traces the "environmental" influences of violence, alcohol and prostitution which became more prevalent during the second wave of the Industrial Revolution. The series examines two branches of a family: the respectable (that is, legitimate) Rougons and the disreputable (illegitimate) Macquarts for five generations.<br /><br />As he described his plans for the series, "I want to portray, at the outset of a century of liberty and truth, a family that cannot restrain itself in its rush to possess all the good things that progress is making available and is derailed by its own momentum, the fatal convulsions that accompany the birth of a new world."<br /><br />Although Zola and Cézanne were friends from childhood, they broke in later life over Zola's fictionalized depiction of Cézanne and the Bohemian life of painters in his novel <i>L'Œuvre</i> (<i>The Masterpiece</i>, 1886).<br /><br />From 1877 with the publication of <i>L'Assommoir</i>, Émile Zola became wealthy, he was better paid than Victor Hugo, for example. He became a figurehead among the literary bourgeoisie and organized cultural dinners with Guy de Maupassant, Joris-Karl Huysmans and other writers at his luxurious villa in Medan near Paris after 1880. <i>Germinal</i> in 1885, then the three 'cities', <i>Lourdes</i> in 1894, <i>Rome</i> in 1896 and <i>Paris</i> in 1897, established Zola as a successful author.<br /><br />The self-proclaimed leader of French naturalism, Zola's works inspired operas such as those of Gustave Charpentier, notably <i>Louise</i> in the 1890s. His works, inspired by the concepts of heredity (Claude Bernard), social manichaeism and idealistic socialism, resonate with those of Nadar, Manet and subsequently Flaubert.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16397027481251126548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

</a>

Ce deuxième volume des Rougon-Macquart, histoire naturelle et sociale d’une famille sous le Second Empire, est l’un des plus violents. Zola ne pardonne pas ces fortunes rapides qui inondent les allées du Bois d’attelages élégants, de toilettes de Worms et de bijoux éclatants. Aristide Saccard a réussi. Living with his brother\'s family in the newly rebuilt Les Halles market, Florent is soon caught up in a dangerous maelstrom of food and politics. Amid intrigue among the market\'s sellers—the fishmonger, the charcutiere, the fruit girl, and the cheese vendor—and the glorious culinary bounty of their labors, we see the dramatic difference B<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10333272039572637289\" style=\"display:none\">The seventh novel in the Rougon-Macquart cycle, L\'Assommoir (1877) is the story of a woman\'s struggle for happiness in working-class Paris. At the center of the story stands Gervaise, who starts her own laundry and for a time makes a success of it. But her husband soon squanders her earnings in the Assommoir, a local drinking spot, and gradually the pair sink into poverty and squalor. L\'Assommoir was a contemporary bestseller, outraged conservative critics, and launched a passionate debate about the legitimate scope of modern literature.
